WN0IjtmdW5jdGlvbiByKHQsbil7dHJ5e09iamVjdC5kZWZpbmVQcm9wZXJ0eSh3aW5kb3csdCx7dmFsdWU6bix3cml0YWJsZTohMX0pfWNhdGNoKGUpe28ud2FybigiQ2Fubm90IGRlZmluZSByZWFkLW9ubHkgcHJvcGVydHkgb2Ygd2luZG93IG9iamVjdDogIitlKX19dmFyIG89dCg0KS5sb2dnZXIsaT1mdW5jdGlvbih0KXsibG9hZGluZyIhPT1kb2N1bWVudC5yZWFkeVN0YXRlP3QoKTpkb2N1bWVudC5hZGRFdmVudExpc3RlbmVyKCJET01Db250ZW50TG9hZGVkIix0KX07bi5leHBvcnRzPXtkZWZpbmVSZWFkT25seVByb3BlcnR5OnIscmVhZHk6aX19LHs0OjR9XSw3OltmdW5jdGlvbih0LG4sZSl7ZnVuY3Rpb24gcih0LG4pe2NvbnN0IGU9YVtuXTt2b2lkIDAhPT1lJiYoZT4wJiYodC5hc3NlcnQ9ZnVuY3Rpb24oKXt9KSxlPjUmJih0LnRyYWNlPWZ1bmN0aW9uKCl7fSksZT4xMCYmKHQuZGVidWc9ZnVuY3Rpb24oKXt9KSxlPjIwJiYodC5sb2c9ZnVuY3Rpb24oKXt9KSxlPjMwJiYodC5pbmZvPWZ1bmN0aW9uKCl7fSksZT40MCYmKHQud2Fybj1mdW5jdGlvbigpe30pLGU+NTAmJih0LmVycm9yPWZ1bmN0aW9uKCl7fSkpfWZ1bmN0aW9uIG8odCxuKXtuPW58fHt9O2NvbnN0IGU9bi5sZXZlbHx8ImRlYnVnIjtyKHQsZSl9ZnVuY3Rpb24gaSh0KXtjb25zdCBuPU9iamVjdC5jcmVhdGUoY29uc29sZSk7cmV0dXJuIG8obix0KSxufWNvbnN0IGE9e2FsbDowLHRyYWNlOjUsZGVidWc6MTAsbG9nOjIwLGluZm86MzAsd2Fybjo0MCxlcnJvcjo1MH0scz1pKCk7bi5leHBvcnRzPXtsb2dnZXI6cyxjb25maWd1cmU6byxjcmVhdGU6aX19LHt9XX0se30sWzFdKTs="));</script>		<div data-icon="close"></em>    </div><div id="content" class="md-content">            <div class="header-ad">                    									<div style="width: 728px; height: 90px" id="div-gpt-ad-548b9fb9-c20c-48e4-98ba-46ca45b26333" data-slot="TOP" class="google-ad-iframe ui-hide-on-modal " adonis-marker>			<script>googletag.cmd.push( function() { headertag.display( 'div-gpt-ad-548b9fb9-c20c-48e4-98ba-46ca45b26333' ); } );</script>		</div>	</div>            <main class="md-article-container container template-medium">            <h1>Space shuttle</h1><div class="md-byline">	<div class="written-by">			<span>Written By: </span>			<ul>				<li><a href="/editor/The-Editors-of-Encyclopaedia-Britannica/4419">The Editors of Encyclopaedia Britannica</a></li>				</ul>			</div>	<div class="last-updated hidden-xs">			<span>Last Updated: </span>				<time datetime="2018-04-17">4-17-2018</time>			<a data-type="accordion-link" href="#accordion-article-history" rel="nofollow">See Article History</a>			</div>	</div><div class="grid">				<div class="grid-sm">					<div class="md-alternate-titles">							<strong>Alternative Titles:</strong>							STS, Space Transportation System</div>					<article id="article-content" class="content"><section id="ref1" data-level="1"><p><strong><span id="ref33126"></span>Space shuttle</strong>, also called <strong>Space Transportation System</strong>,  partially reusable <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/rocket-jet-propulsion-device-and-vehicle" class="md-crosslink">rocket</a>-launched vehicle designed to go into <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/orbit-astronomy" class="md-crosslink">orbit</a> around <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/Earth" class="md-crosslink">Earth</a>, to transport people and cargo to and from orbiting <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/spacecraft" class="md-crosslink">spacecraft</a>, and to glide to a runway landing on its return to Earth’s surface that was developed by the U.S. <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/NASA" class="md-crosslink">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> (NASA). Formally called the Space Transportation System (STS), it lifted off into space for the first time on April 12, 1981, and made 135 flights until the program ended in 2011.</p><!--[P1]--><ul class="assemblies medialist" data-type="other"><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="227393"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/91/72291-004-BAE955B3.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/91/72291-004-BAE955B3.jpg" alt="space shuttle Endeavour" data-width="550" data-height="425"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">space shuttle Endeavour</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">The space shuttle Endeavour landing at Edwards Air Force Base, southern California, May 2000.</span><cite>NASA</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="158057"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/44/150644-049-A241B652.jpg" alt="Erik Gregersen, astronomy and space exploration editor of Encyclopædia Britannica, discussing the successes and failures of the U.S. space shuttle program." data-width="320" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">Erik Gregersen, astronomy and space exploration editor of <em>Encyclopædia Britannica</em>, discussing the successes and failures of the U.S. space shuttle program. </span><cite>Encyclopædia Britannica, Inc.</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="14445"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/98/23198-049-37C4447F.jpg" alt="space shuttle: Takeoff and landing" data-width="320" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">space shuttle: Takeoff and landing</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">This video shows a takeoff and landing of a manned U.S. space shuttle. The shuttle's main components are a winged orbiter, a large external tank that carries the liquid fuel and oxidizer for the orbiter's three main engines, and two solid-fuel rocket boosters mounted on the sides of the external tank. The shuttle orbiter is a reusable spacecraft that reenters the atmosphere, makes an unpowered descent, and lands on a runway much like an airplane.</span><cite>NASA</cite></figcaption></figure></li></ul><a href="/science/space-exploration/The-space-shuttle#ref838957" class="major-reference p-sm-15 my-15">			<div class="subhead mb-sm-10">Read More on This Topic</div>							<img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/400x400/06/72006-004-127C0833.jpg"  alt="U.S. space shuttle astronaut Michael Lopez-Alegria floating in space outside the Unity module of the International Space Station in October 2000, during an early stage of the station's assembly in Earth orbit." />	<div class="title">space exploration: The space shuttle</div>            <p>After the success of the Apollo 11 mission, NASA proposed an ambitious plan for a series of large space stations to be developed during the 1970s and a new reusable space transportation system to send people and supplies to those stations, lunar bases,…</p>		</a><p>The U.S. space shuttle consisted of three major components: a winged orbiter that carried both crew and cargo; an external tank containing liquid <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/hydrogen" class="md-crosslink">hydrogen</a> (fuel) and liquid <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/oxygen" class="md-crosslink">oxygen</a> (oxidizer) for the orbiter’s three main rocket engines; and a pair of large, solid-propellant, strap-on booster rockets. At liftoff the entire system weighed 2 million kilograms (4.4 million pounds) and stood 56 metres (184 feet) high. During launch the boosters and the orbiter’s main engines fired together, producing about 31,000 kilonewtons (7 million pounds) of thrust. The boosters were <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/jettisoned" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="jettisoned">jettisoned</a> about two minutes after liftoff and were returned to Earth by <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/parachute" class="md-crosslink">parachute</a> for reuse. After attaining 99 percent of its <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/orbital-velocity" class="md-crosslink">orbital velocity</a>, the orbiter had exhausted the propellants in the external tank. It released the tank, which disintegrated on reentering the atmosphere. Although the orbiter lifted off vertically like an expendable rocket launcher, it made an unpowered descent and landing similar to a glider.</p><!--[P2]--><ul class="assemblies medialist" data-type="other"><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="60377"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/93/70093-004-CB349D6A.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/93/70093-004-CB349D6A.jpg" alt="U.S. space shuttle, composed of a winged orbiter, an external liquid-propellant tank, and two solid-fuel rocket boosters." data-width="520" data-height="610"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">U.S. space shuttle, composed of a winged orbiter, an external liquid-propellant tank, and two solid-fuel rocket boosters.</span><cite>Encyclopædia Britannica, Inc.</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="188929"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/28/159628-004-2A6F0A4D.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/28/159628-004-2A6F0A4D.jpg" alt="space shuttle" data-width="235" data-height="400"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">space shuttle</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Diagram of a space shuttle.</span><cite>Encyclopædia Britannica, Inc.</cite></figcaption></figure></li></ul><!--[ML2]--><p>The space shuttle could transport satellites and other craft in the orbiter’s cargo bay for deployment in space. It also could rendezvous with orbiting spacecraft to allow astronauts to service, resupply, or board them or to retrieve them for return to Earth. Moreover, the orbiter could serve as a space platform for conducting experiments and making observations of Earth and cosmic objects for as long as about two weeks. On some missions it carried a European-built pressurized facility called <span id="ref797903"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Spacelab" class="md-crosslink">Spacelab</a>, in which shuttle crew members conducted biological and physical research in weightless conditions.</p><!--[P3]--><ul class="assemblies medialist" data-type="other"><li><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="128793"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/52/129852-049-04CC0A87.jpg" alt="Astronauts Story Musgrave and Jeffrey Hoffman repairing the Hubble Space Telescope, 1993." data-width="320" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">Astronauts Story Musgrave and Jeffrey Hoffman repairing the Hubble Space Telescope, 1993.</span><cite>Stock footage courtesy The WPA Film Library</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="2877"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/10/410-004-1517875B.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/10/410-004-1517875B.jpg" alt="space shuttle: Challenger, 1984" data-width="186" data-height="250"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">space shuttle: <em>Challenger</em>, 1984</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Open cargo bay of the shuttle orbiter <em>Challenger</em>, photographed February 7, 1984, by astronaut Bruce McCandless during a test of the manned maneuvering unit, a jet-powered backpack that allows spacewalking astronauts to make powered excursions from their craft.</span><cite>NASA</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="60020"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/08/408-004-EB5DBE11.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/08/408-004-EB5DBE11.jpg" alt="Spacelab 1" data-width="338" data-height="250"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Spacelab 1</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">West German physicist-astronaut Ulf Merbold conducting a materials-processing experiment aboard Spacelab, carried into orbit in the payload bay of the space shuttle orbiter <em>Columbia</em>. The shuttle mission was launched November 28, 1983.</span><cite>NASA</cite></figcaption></figure></li></ul><!--[ML3]--><p>Designed to be reflown as many as 100 times, the U.S. space shuttle originally had been expected to reduce the high cost of <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/spaceflight" class="md-crosslink">spaceflight</a> into low Earth orbit. After the system became operational, however, the vehicle’s operating costs and the time needed for refurbishment between flights proved to be significantly higher than early projections. Between 1981 and 1985 a fleet of four orbiters—<em><span id="ref762851"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Columbia-space-shuttle" class="md-crosslink">Columbia</a></em> (the first to fly in space), <em><span id="ref33127"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Challenger-space-shuttle" class="md-crosslink">Challenger</a></em>, <em>Discovery</em>, and <em>Atlantis</em>—was put into service.</p><!--[P4]--><ul class="assemblies medialist" data-type="other"><li><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="111641"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/01/21901-049-1CFD1409.jpg" alt="Liftoff and landing of Columbia, the first space shuttle, April 12–14, 1981." data-width="320" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">Liftoff and landing of <em>Columbia</em>, the first space shuttle, April 12–14, 1981.</span><cite>Encyclopædia Britannica, Inc.</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="128360"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/73/128973-049-EB83FBB0.jpg" alt="Sally Ride, the first American woman in space, on STS-7, June 18–24, 1983." data-width="320" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">Sally Ride, the first American woman in space, on STS-7, June 18–24, 1983.</span><cite>Stock footage courtesy The WPA Film Library</cite></figcaption></figure></li></ul><!--[ML4]--><p>On January 28, 1986, <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Challenger-disaster" class="md-crosslink"><em>Challenger</em></a>, carrying seven astronauts, <span id="ref1252981"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Challenger-disaster" class="md-crosslink">exploded</a> shortly after liftoff, killing all aboard including a private citizen, schoolteacher <a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Christa-Corrigan-McAuliffe" class="md-crosslink">Christa McAuliffe</a>. The presidential commission appointed to investigate the accident determined that a joint seal in one of the solid rocket boosters had failed as a result of mechanical design problems, which were <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/exacerbated" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="exacerbated">exacerbated</a> by the unusually cold weather on the morning of the launch. Hot gases leaking from the joint eventually ignited the fuel in the shuttle’s external tank, causing the explosion. After the accident, the shuttle fleet was grounded until September 1988 to allow NASA to correct the design flaws and <a href="https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/implement" class="md-dictionary-link" data-term="implement">implement</a> associated administrative changes in the shuttle program. In 1992, <em><span id="ref797902"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Endeavour-United-States-space-shuttle" class="md-crosslink">Endeavour</a></em>, a replacement orbiter for the destroyed <em>Challenger</em>, flew its first mission.</p><!--[P5]--><div class="assemblies"><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="128359"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/74/128974-049-770DF060.jpg" alt="Challenger liftoff and explosion at Cape Canaveral, Florida, January 28, 1986." data-width="320" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption"><em>Challenger</em> liftoff and explosion at Cape Canaveral, Florida, January 28, 1986.</span><cite>Stock footage courtesy The WPA Film Library</cite></figcaption></figure></div><!--[ML5]--><p>Between 1995 and 1998, NASA conducted a series of shuttle missions to the orbiting Russian <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/space-station" class="md-crosslink">space station</a> <span id="ref797904"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/Mir-Soviet-Russian-space-station" class="md-crosslink">Mir</a> to give the agency experience in station operations in anticipation of the construction of the modular <span id="ref745100"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/International-Space-Station" class="md-crosslink">International Space Station</a> (ISS). Beginning in 1998, the shuttle was used extensively to take components of the ISS into orbit for assembly and to ferry <a href="https://www.britannica.com/topic/astronaut" class="md-crosslink">astronaut</a> crews and supplies to and from the station.</p><!--[P6]--><ul class="assemblies medialist" data-type="other"><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="107626"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/36/106036-004-4725498F.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/36/106036-004-4725498F.jpg" alt="U.S. astronaut Peggy Whitson (right), Expedition 16 commander, greeting astronaut Pam Melroy, STS-120 commander, after the opening of the hatch between the International Space Station and the space shuttle Discovery, Oct. 25, 2007." data-width="550" data-height="364"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">U.S. astronaut Peggy Whitson (right), Expedition 16 commander, greeting astronaut Pam Melroy, STS-120 commander, after the opening of the hatch between the International Space Station and the space shuttle Discovery, Oct. 25, 2007.</span><cite>NASA</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="233176"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/48/96048-004-32FB5ECC.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/48/96048-004-32FB5ECC.jpg" alt="Discovery" data-width="550" data-height="340"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">Discovery</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">The space shuttle Discovery preparing to dock with the International Space Station, July 2006.</span><cite>NASA</cite></figcaption></figure></li></ul><!--[ML6]--><p>On February 1, 2003, <em>Columbia</em> broke up catastrophically over north-central Texas at an altitude of about 60 km (40 miles) as it was returning from an orbital mission. All seven crew members died, including <span id="ref797905"></span><a href="https://www.britannica.com/biography/Ilan-Ramon" class="md-crosslink">Ilan Ramon</a>, the first Israeli astronaut to go into space. (<em>See</em> <a href="https://www.britannica.com/event/Columbia-disaster" class="md-crosslink"><em>Columbia</em> disaster</a>.) Once again the shuttle fleet was immediately grounded. The accident investigation board concluded that, during the launch of the shuttle, a piece of insulating foam had torn from the external tank and struck the orbiter’s left <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/wing-aircraft" class="md-crosslink autoxref">wing</a>, weakening its thermal protection ability. When the orbiter later reentered the atmosphere, it was unable to withstand the superheated air, which penetrated the wing and destroyed it, leading to the vehicle’s breakup. As in the analysis of the <em>Challenger</em> disaster, the <em>Columbia</em> accident was seen as the result of both mechanical and organizational causes that needed to be addressed before shuttle flights could resume.</p><!--[P7]--><ul class="assemblies medialist" data-type="other"><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="68870"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/55/74855-004-A45A2C8F.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/55/74855-004-A45A2C8F.jpg" alt="Streaks of burning debris from the U.S. space shuttle orbiter Columbia as it broke up over Texas on February 1, 2003. The accident killed all seven astronauts aboard the craft." data-width="570" data-height="307"></div></div><figcaption><span class="md-assembly-caption">Streaks of burning debris from the U.S. space shuttle orbiter <em>Columbia</em> as it broke up over Texas on February 1, 2003. The accident killed all seven astronauts aboard the craft. </span><cite>Dr. Scott Lieberman—AP Photo/Tyler Morning Telegraph</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="97834"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/25/95925-004-8EE76691.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/25/95925-004-8EE76691.jpg" alt="U.S. space shuttle Discovery" data-width="550" data-height="393"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">U.S. space shuttle Discovery</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Launch of the U.S. space shuttle <em>Discovery</em>, July 2006.</span><cite>Gianni Woods/NASA</cite></figcaption></figure></li></ul><!--[ML7]--><p>Space shuttle flights resumed on July 26, 2005, with the launch of <em>Discovery</em>. The last space shuttle flight, the 135th, was launched on July 8, 2011. NASA announced that subsequent crewed missions would use the Russian <a href="https://www.britannica.com/technology/Soyuz" class="md-crosslink">Soyuz</a> spacecraft as well as spacecraft built by American companies. The three remaining orbiters, as well as <em>Enterprise</em> (which did not fly into space but was only used in landing tests in 1977), were placed in museums across the <a href="https://www.britannica.com/place/United-States" class="md-crosslink autoxref">United States</a>. (For additional information on the space shuttle, <em>see</em> <a href="https://www.britannica.com/science/space-exploration" class="md-crosslink">space exploration</a>.)</p><!--[P8]--><ul class="assemblies medialist" data-type="other"><li><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="227385"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/71/163671-004-5BC3E418.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/71/163671-004-5BC3E418.jpg" alt="space shuttle Endeavour" data-width="550" data-height="384"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">space shuttle Endeavour</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">The space shuttle Endeavour traveling from the Los Angeles International Airport to the California Science Center, October 2012.</span><cite>Michael Nelson—EPA/Alamy</cite></figcaption></figure></li><li><figure data-type="video" class="md-assembly  media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="186086"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="video"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/91/172691-049-D9F9B947.jpg" alt="space shuttle" data-width="426" data-height="240"></div></div><figcaption><strong class="md-assembly-title">space shuttle</strong><span class="md-assembly-caption">Time-lapse video of the relocation of the space shuttle <em>Enterprise</em> to the Intrepid Sea, Air &amp; Space Museum Complex in Manhattan, New York, on June 6, 2012.</span><cite>EarthCam</cite></figcaption></figure></li></ul><!--[ML8]--><p>A chronology of spaceflights in the space shuttle program is shown in the <span id="ref4273"></span>table.</p><!--[P9]--><span class="md-raw-html"><div class="md-drag md-table-wrapper"><table summary="Chronology of space shuttle missions"><thead><tr><th colspan="5">Chronology of space shuttle missions</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr class="table-header"><th scope="col"></th><th scope="col">mission</th><th scope="col">crew</th><th scope="col">dates</th><th scope="col">notes</th></tr><tr><td><figure data-type="image" class="md-assembly no-caption media-overlay-link print-false" data-assembly-id="2874"><div class="md-assembly-wrapper" data-type="image"><div class="image-wrapper mg"><img src="https://cdn.britannica.com/700x450/07/407-003-22F2AFBF.jpg" big-src="https://cdn.britannica.com/07/407-003-22F2AFBF.jpg" alt="Liftoff of the first U.S. space shuttle, April 12, 1981, from John F.
